    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer12711634606269910573"><i>A Lover's Discourse</i>, at its 1978 publication, was revolutionary: Roland Barthes made unprecedented use of the tools of structuralism to explore the whimsical phenomenon of love. Rich with references ranging from Goethe's <i>Werther</i> to Winnicott, from Plato to Proust, from Baudelaire to Schubert, <i>A Lover's Discourse</i> artfully draws a portrait in which every reader will find ech</span>
  <span id="freeText12711634606269910573" style="display:none"><i>A Lover's Discourse</i>, at its 1978 publication, was revolutionary: Roland Barthes made unprecedented use of the tools of structuralism to explore the whimsical phenomenon of love. Rich with references ranging from Goethe's <i>Werther</i> to Winnicott, from Plato to Proust, from Baudelaire to Schubert, <i>A Lover's Discourse</i> artfully draws a portrait in which every reader will find echoes of themselves.</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2623039278" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2623039278" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2623039278?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=essays">essays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2623039278" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14792872492075235336">If there is any such thing as a good headache, then Roland Barthes has been successful in giving me one. This was a heavy (no, not the weight of the book, just heavy going, but in grandiose way!), irrefutable, and intense read, where, with the recreation of the lover's fevered consciousness he goes about deconstructing love, to write maybe the the most detailed, painstaking anatomy of desire that we are ever likely to see. Simply put, these are his thoughts on love, in the form of short essays, </span>
  <span id="freeText14792872492075235336" style="display:none">If there is any such thing as a good headache, then Roland Barthes has been successful in giving me one. This was a heavy (no, not the weight of the book, just heavy going, but in grandiose way!), irrefutable, and intense read, where, with the recreation of the lover's fevered consciousness he goes about deconstructing love, to write maybe the the most detailed, painstaking anatomy of desire that we are ever likely to see. Simply put, these are his thoughts on love, in the form of short essays, each one covering the many different aspects of the romantic life. Whether falling in, painfully letting go, or being completely smitten, head over heels in love, Barthes covers it.<br /><br />After each scene is formulated, Barthes subjects it to a philosophical battering of vigorous analysis, that constantly adds references from literary sources such as Goethe, Nietzsche, Freud, and Rilke, whilst throwing psychological and linguistic perspectives into the mix as well. Although flowing for the most part with a stream-of-consciousness, that does feel dense, and a little self indulgent, there can be no doubt as to its effectiveness throughout. There was an emotional power to his prose, that, for anyone that ever loved, may be reminded, and forced to face up to moments from their own intimate past.<br /><br />Although this does require much mental effort that really sends one's grey matter into overdrive (especially for those not accustomed with philosophical writings) Barthes strikes a cord deep within with a study of love that is subtle, rich in insight, penetrating the heart as well as the head. Barthes breaks down the human experience of love so effortlessly, but I'm not sure this led me to better understand love, as everyone has their own ways of perceiving it. This was a beautiful and thought provoking read though, that was a pure delight to explore.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14792872492075235336" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_480902516" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/480902516" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6971227-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564001692p2/6971227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/480902516?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/6971227-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374532314" href="/book/show/8270125-a-lover-s-discourse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6971227-david?shelf=essays-theory">essays-theory</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6971227-david?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer480902516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1544935214322091240">"Love" seems to me something which is impossible to define, to grasp. Centuries of authors, of philosophers, have tried to do so in vain. There is always something left to be said. As in death, love is a topic of infinite discourse. As Tolstoy echoes in the mouth of <i>Anna Karenina</i>'s titular heroine: "<i>'I think... if there are as many minds as there are men, then there are as many kinds of love as there are hearts.'</i>" Love is infinite in it's permutations, and therefore cannot be defined. What Barth</span>
  <span id="freeText1544935214322091240" style="display:none">"Love" seems to me something which is impossible to define, to grasp. Centuries of authors, of philosophers, have tried to do so in vain. There is always something left to be said. As in death, love is a topic of infinite discourse. As Tolstoy echoes in the mouth of <i>Anna Karenina</i>'s titular heroine: "<i>'I think... if there are as many minds as there are men, then there are as many kinds of love as there are hearts.'</i>" Love is infinite in it's permutations, and therefore cannot be defined. What Barthes offers is not a definition of Love, but what it is to be a Lover. Barthes, like his predecessors Proust, Shakespeare, Ovid, Baudelaire, Goethe, Stendhal, etc., is a troubadour of the pains of jealousy disguised as the joys of love. <i>A Lover's Discourse</i> is a masterful fugue of personal experience, literary precedence, and theoretical musing, which evokes emotion in the same pitch as a novel, but elicits introspection with the intellectual skepticism of <i>Hamlet</i>.<br /><br />As a piece representative of the Barthesian oeuvre, <i>A Lover's Discourse</i> straddles the duality of speech and meaning, of what it means to be a lover, but also the very <i>discourse</i> of love. The book itself is divided pell-mell into short fragments related to the amorous phraseology: "<i>s'abîmer...,</i>" "<i>cœur,</i>" "<i>casés...</i>" etc. It is the layered language of love which interests Barthes: what do we say when we are in love? - is what we intend what we say? - what does what we say really <i>mean</i>, what does it <i>signify</i>? Though the semiotic approach to love seems distant and cold, it is the inverse which we feel when reading Barthes, whose very language moves the reader to a shudder of feeling: <i>
  <blockquote>Am I in love? --yes, since I am waiting. The other one never waits. Sometimes I want to play the part of the one who doesn't wait; I try to busy myself elsewhere, to arrive late; but I always lose at this game. Whatever I do, I find myself there, with nothing to do, punctual, even ahead of time. The lover's fatal identity is precisely this: I am the one who waits.</blockquote>
</i>Perhaps this book, novelistic essay or essayistic novel, must be read in one's prime, when one is in the throes of passion, to feel the full emotional impact - I do not know if this is the case. As a young man I am always on the precipice of romantic disaster, only in utter solitude, removed from all passionate enterprises, do I feel free from the pharmacopoeia (half-poison, half-remedy) of love. Bliss and misery are the Janus faces of life, in love, in solitude, we cannot have one without the other, even if they only look at us in turns.<i>
  <blockquote>The world subjects every enterprise to an alternative; that of success or failure, of victory or defeat... Flouted in my enterprise (as it happens), I emerge from it neither victor nor vanquished: I am tragic.</blockquote>
</i>Love, life, and death, are infinite, they are the lands of contradictions, beyond the capacity of language. What is both bliss and misery? What is the concatenation of victory and failure? How does die and yet endure? At these interstices of language lies the fundamental truths of Love.<br /><br />What does it mean to be in love? It is a notion idealized and raised on high by all men, it is the apparent culmination of our lives. But with Love comes pain. For Barthes Love is inseparable from Jealousy: if we are not jealous, it diminishes our love, it negates it. We can never be happy in love, never truly happy, never complaisant. The lover is always waiting, he must ever have his love validated, requited, and won. Every win in love is a Pyrrhic victory, every favor won is hours, days, of agony paid for. This is the view which Barthes takes, but it is not his argument. His view of love is a flavor of <i>A Lover's Discourse</i>, but it is not the entire course. What do we mean when we declare the object of our love "adorable"? What do we mean when we affirm our love? These are the concerns of Barthes. "What do we mean when we are in love?" no "what do we mean when we say 'I am in love'?"<br /><br />The question of <i>A Lover's Discourse</i> is not "how does one define love?" but rather, more fundamentally, how does one even begin to discuss it? When we read the Romantics, Byron, Keats, Shelley, we are presented with a view of Love that seems too large, too incompatible with feeble man: something more withheld from man for his imperfections, something which is manifest as a remote deity. Contrarily, when we discuss it in the quotidian tongue, it seems to us too pale a light: it lacks the allure of passion, something is missing. Despite his apotheosis of Language, even Barthes feels its inadequacy in front of the edifice of Love: <i>
  <blockquote>To try to write love is to confront the muck of language; that region of hysteria where language is both too much and too little, excessive (by the limitless expansion of the ego, by emotive submersion) and impoverished (by the codes on which love diminishes and levels it).</blockquote>
</i>In front of Love, language is reduced to muck, it is inadequate. Barthes is torn between the deities of <i>Eros</i> and <i>Logos</i> - Love and Language. As a humbled votary genuflecting to the altar of Language, he is prostrate before the temple of Love.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1544935214322091240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2388059701" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10694651633631163795">A witty analysis of the experience and language of love, unfolding in a series of fast-paced fragments considering everything from what it means to be engulfed in passion to the tropes of the love letter. Across entries Barthes refers to and comments on what famous authors have written on the subject, engaging with their sundry thoughts as he develops his own.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_12134794" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12134794" />
    <a title="Alan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/318068-alan"><img alt="Alan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1350575041p2/318068.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12134794?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan" href="/user/show/318068-alan">Alan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099437420" href="/book/show/248326.A_Lover_s_Discourse">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovesick</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Alan by: </span>
      a lovesick
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/318068-alan?shelf=lost-while-reading">lost-while-reading</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12134794" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3273050996696774617">Fuck! Left in random Manhattan apt, then shipped to Haiti in aunt's luggage.<br/><br/>-----<br/><br/>Double fuck! Lost it again on the subway with hundreds of notes.<br/><br/>-----<br/><br/>Ok finished, after 6 months. <br/><br/>This book is a destroying and destroyed queer love poem masquerading half-assedly as theory. It is a poem with a mustache of theory. And it's pretty great for this. He sets it up as aspiring to decode a liminal site of discourse: the lover's discourse "is completely forsaken by the surrounding languages: ignored, dispar</span>
  <span id="freeText3273050996696774617" style="display:none">Fuck! Left in random Manhattan apt, then shipped to Haiti in aunt's luggage.<br /><br />-----<br /><br />Double fuck! Lost it again on the subway with hundreds of notes.<br /><br />-----<br /><br />Ok finished, after 6 months. <br /><br />This book is a destroying and destroyed queer love poem masquerading half-assedly as theory. It is a poem with a mustache of theory. And it's pretty great for this. He sets it up as aspiring to decode a liminal site of discourse: the lover's discourse "is completely forsaken by the surrounding languages: ignored, disparaged, or derided by them."--and does this in a way that means to be understood for its universality. But then he clearly makes no bones about describing sitting by the phone in coldsweats gnawing (his own) fingers and desolate, waiting for "X" to call him. This is charming and sweet.<br /><br />More importantly, the book is just incredibly brilliant, and just true. He positions the simple act of recognition, the utterance: "That is so true..." as the qualifier for an amorous image to be constitutive of the lover's "image repertoire"(as he calls it). Most all of his images qualify in this regard; they are immediately recognizable (to me at least). E.g., this illustration from the entry "Monstrous." "The lover's discourse stifles the other, who finds no place for his own language beneath this massive utterance."<br /><br />The book is divided, seemingly haphazardly (alphabetically), into sections dealing with various utterances, conditions, or dispositions of the amorous image repertoire. Absence, adorable, affirmation, alteration, etc.<br /><br />But really the book should be called An Unrequited Lover's Discourse, because it has *nothing* to do with the discourses or image repertoire that arise on love fulfilled. *That* discourse comes out the other end of the book as the only remaining liminal site of the "disparaged" lovers discourse. It is as though Barthes' personal loss is so palpable, so in need of codification in theory, of respect, that it elides the possibility of requitement altogether, positioning loss as the totality of love. A *romantic* position to be sure, and one not altogether out of step with *The Sorrows of Young Wether*, the major source text here (among a great many others). <br /><br />But above all, really, is the simple fact that I could read a thousand pages of Barthes describing a single, unremarkable turd and be satisfied. He has a Nietzschean disposition toward cataclysm and provocation, toward paradox and the bending of incompetent languages around his meaning--he digs impertinently, surgically, for the actual in a way that would seem exclusive with such gentle taste--he is generous and lovable (unvikinglike) in a way that Nietzsche isn't (in the way that Rilke or e e cummings *are*).<br /><br />Good parts from the first half:<br /><br />"Meaning (destiny) electrifies my hand; I am about to tear open the other's opaque body, oblige the other (whether there is a response, a withdrawal, or mere acceptance) to enter into the interplay of meaning: I am about *to make the other speak*."<br /><br />"Language is a skin: I rub my language against the other. It is as if I had words instead of fingers, or fingers at the tip of my words. My language trembles with desire. The emotion derives from a double contact: on the one hand, a whole activity of discourse discreetly, indirectly focuses upon a single signified, which is "I desire you," and releases, nourishes, ramifies it to the point of explosion (language experiences orgasm upon touching itself); on the other hand I enwrap the other in my words, I caress, brush against, talk up this contact, I extend myself to make the commentary to which I submit the relation endure."<br /><br />"To speak amorously is to expend without an end in sight, without a *crisis;*..."<br /><br />"...any ethic of purity requires that we detach the gift from the hand which gives or receives it..."<br /><br />"To speak of the gift is to place it in an exchange economy (of sacrifice, competition, etc.); which stands opposed to silent expenditure."<br /><br />"Nature, today, is the city."<br /><br />"The mechanics of amorous vassalage require a fathomless futility."</span>
  <a data-text-id="3273050996696774617" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1229234894" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1229234894?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374532314" href="/book/show/8270125-a-lover-s-discourse">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">you who philosophize over love and life's disharmony</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=french-oeuvre">french-oeuvre</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1229234894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10352213329455719886"><i>
  <blockquote>
    <b>Language is a skin: I rub my language against the other. It is as if words instead of fingers, or fingers at the tip of my words. My language trembles with desire. </b>
  </blockquote>
</i><br/>- This is a book you either read over a period of time, in spurts, in fragments as it is written, or you binge read in a couple of days, like I have. Each chapter is a definition, a philosophical tease, a shortened version of what could be a lecture or an erudite discussion on life and love; after all, Barthes made his liv</span>
  <span id="freeText10352213329455719886" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>
    <b>Language is a skin: I rub my language against the other. It is as if words instead of fingers, or fingers at the tip of my words. My language trembles with desire. </b>
  </blockquote>
</i><br />- This is a book you either read over a period of time, in spurts, in fragments as it is written, or you binge read in a couple of days, like I have. Each chapter is a definition, a philosophical tease, a shortened version of what could be a lecture or an erudite discussion on life and love; after all, Barthes made his living as an academic.<br /><br />- This is a book you should read after having read Goethe's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16641.The_Sorrows_of_Young_Werther_and_Selected_Writings__Signet_Classics_" title="The Sorrows of Young Werther and Selected Writings (Signet Classics) by Johann Wolfgang von Goethe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sorrows of Young Werther and Selected Writings</a>. A few notable ones are mentioned in Barthes' <i>A Lover's Discourse</i>: Freud, Proust, and Nietzsche. However, it is a comparative study of Goethe's <i>Werther</i> and his stance on his love, or I should say, his helplessness because of love. (However, read <i>The Selected Writings</i> version of <i>Young Werther</i> and you'll learn, from Goethe himself, that this feeling of despair started before love, that love may have been a trigger, yes, but, according to Goethe, most readers tend to evaluate the book differently).<br /><br />- This is a book to read only when you're open to discussing love in several abstract and concrete forms. Seriously, how many ways can we talk about love? The theories are endless, so it's no surprise that this becomes an anatomy of lust and love, of the essence and the reality of love; or as Barthes puts it, the disreal and unreal, the cosmos. <br /><i>
  <blockquote>
    <b>To try to write love is to confront the muck of language: that region of hysteria where language is both too much and too little, excessive.</b>
  </blockquote>
</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="10352213329455719886" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_46905243" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/46905243" />
    <a title="Denis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1729623-denis"><img alt="Denis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1227151520p2/1729623.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/46905243?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Denis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Denis" href="/user/show/1729623-denis">Denis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer46905243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10922506851697202432">This book is a classic in France, and it's probably Barthe's most popular work. It is absolutely brilliant, and may be well be the best analysis ever made of love, as seen from the beginning to the end of a relationship. It isn't a novel, it's not an essay either, nor a self-help book or a psychology study: it's just, as the title implies, fragments - fragments about the daily life of two people in love, people at various stage of love, and those fragments capture so perfectly, so intimately, so</span>
  <span id="freeText10922506851697202432" style="display:none">This book is a classic in France, and it's probably Barthe's most popular work. It is absolutely brilliant, and may be well be the best analysis ever made of love, as seen from the beginning to the end of a relationship. It isn't a novel, it's not an essay either, nor a self-help book or a psychology study: it's just, as the title implies, fragments - fragments about the daily life of two people in love, people at various stage of love, and those fragments capture so perfectly, so intimately, so precisely all the different aspects of love, that their totality forms a universal, transcendent, and mesmerizing vision of what love between two human beings can be. It is bittersweet, in the sense that the course of love is always almost the same, yet it's a book filled with happiness, joys, and at the end quite reassuring: what ever heartbreak you've been through, it's finally quite normal. Reading this book won't teach anyone how to love better or more wisely, but it does portray the complexities, small and big, and the mutliple wonders of love, in a very unique and direct way. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10922506851697202432" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13652254034165371077">I first read, and fell in love, with Roland Barthes at uni. Christ, I was still a virgin when I swooned over ALD for the first time. Now at the tail-end of a long relationship, the terrible beauty of Barthes' writing is quite effulgent.<br/><br/>I was reminded again of how great a novel (well, anti-novel...) ALD is when Jeffrey Eugenides paid such tender, bittersweet homage to it in 'The Marriage Plot'.<br/><br/>There is a scene where Madeleine is lying in bed reading The Book, eating peanut butter from the jar wit</span>
  <span id="freeText13652254034165371077" style="display:none">I first read, and fell in love, with Roland Barthes at uni. Christ, I was still a virgin when I swooned over ALD for the first time. Now at the tail-end of a long relationship, the terrible beauty of Barthes' writing is quite effulgent.<br /><br />I was reminded again of how great a novel (well, anti-novel...) ALD is when Jeffrey Eugenides paid such tender, bittersweet homage to it in 'The Marriage Plot'.<br /><br />There is a scene where Madeleine is lying in bed reading The Book, eating peanut butter from the jar with a spoon, while it is raining outside ... My God, how romantic is that!<br /><br />"A moment of affirmation; for a certain time, though a finite one, a deranged interval, something has been successful: I have been fulfilled (all my desires abolished by the plenitude of their satisfaction): fulfillment does exist, and I shall keep on making it return: through all the meanderings of my amorous history, I shall persist in wanting to rediscover ..."<br /><br />The above quote is from a section called 'In the loving comfort of your arms'. Who needs Oprah Winfrey, as bland as processed Big Mac cheese, when you can have the Holy Emmental (elemental?) Barthes to comfort, distract and chafe you simultaneously?<br /><br />In the canon of greatest literature about love, ALD is up there with 'Song of Songs' and the 'Kama Sutra'.<br /><br />A book to live and love by.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5612069383657095638">a lengthy set of scenarios evidencing our inability to speak the full truth of our loves as a result of the drive's inevitable detours through the defiles of the signifier. i have no idea why so many people find it erotic or expressive of their most intimate amorous sentiments. if anything, the book strikes a poignant note insofar as it amasses example after example of how the imaginary (our desires) and the symbolic (our words and concepts) inevitably fail to match one another. it occurs to me </span>
  <span id="freeText5612069383657095638" style="display:none">a lengthy set of scenarios evidencing our inability to speak the full truth of our loves as a result of the drive's inevitable detours through the defiles of the signifier. i have no idea why so many people find it erotic or expressive of their most intimate amorous sentiments. if anything, the book strikes a poignant note insofar as it amasses example after example of how the imaginary (our desires) and the symbolic (our words and concepts) inevitably fail to match one another. it occurs to me after reading various other reviews, that people should spend far less time projecting their fantasies on to authors and titles, and far more time reading books with the same care that went into writing them.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374532314" href="/book/show/8270125-a-lover-s-discourse">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Angus, Bennard</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4111021-monique?shelf=five-stars">five-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4111021-monique?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4111021-monique?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4111021-monique?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4111021-monique?shelf=love">love</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer537766113" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11238652276559656670"><br/>Originally posted <a href='http://bookishlittleme.attymonique.com/2013/03/a-lovers-discourse-fragments.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380409907i/803069._SY540_.jpg' width='300' height='300' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Admittedly, this is the kind of book that I will quickly chuck for its verbosity. I’ve always thought books like this – those that use hemorrhagic and florid words – were written more for the purpose of exhibiting the author’s unparalleled vocabulary more than anything. But for some reason, I hung on to this one. I stayed with it, and it stayed with me. Willingly. <br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>Language is a skin: I rub my language against the other. It is as if I had words instead of fingers, or fin</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText11238652276559656670" style="display:none"><br>Originally posted <a href="http://bookishlittleme.attymonique.com/2013/03/a-lovers-discourse-fragments.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380409907i/803069._SY540_.jpg" width="300" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Admittedly, this is the kind of book that I will quickly chuck for its verbosity. I’ve always thought books like this – those that use hemorrhagic and florid words – were written more for the purpose of exhibiting the author’s unparalleled vocabulary more than anything. But for some reason, I hung on to this one. I stayed with it, and it stayed with me. Willingly. <br><br><blockquote>
  <i>Language is a skin: I rub my language against the other. It is as if I had words instead of fingers, or fingers at the tip of my words. My language trembles with desire. The emotion derives from a double contact: on the one hand, a whole activity of discourse discreetly, indirectly focuses upon a single signified, which is "I desire you," and releases, nourishes, ramifies it to the point of explosion (language experiences orgasm upon touching itself); on the other hand, I enwrap the other in my words, I caress, brush against, talk up this contact, I extend myself to make the commentary to which I submit the relation endure.</i>
</blockquote><br><br>These fragments are the marriage of love and theory – <i>love theorized</i>. Barthes’ brilliance is beyond cavil. I should have picked it up after Jeffrey Eugenides paid tribute to it through Madeleine in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10964693-the-marriage-plot" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Marriage Plot</i>
</a>, and why I didn’t now escapes me. <br><br>Barthes assigns names to people, places and things which he makes use of throughout the book. “The other” or the "amorous subject" is the loved one, the subject of the speaker’s affections. “Amorous desire” is the feeling of love from speaker to “the other.” The speaker is alternately male and female. And while Barthes cites references constantly, it won’t matter that you haven’t a clue what it is – <i>who the hell is Goethe?</i> – focus on the text, on the fragments, and it will make perfect sense. <br><br><blockquote>
  <i>How does a love end?-- Then it does end? To tell the truth, no one--except for the others-- ever knows anything about it; a kind of innocence conceals the end of this thing conceived, asserted, lived according to eternity. Whatever the loved being becomes, whether he vanishes or moves into the realm of Friendship, in any case I never see him disappear; the love which is over and done with passes into another world  like a ship into space, lights no longer winking: the loved being once echoed loudly, now that being is entirely without resonance (the other never disappears when and how we expect). This phenomenon results from a constraint in the lover's discourse: I myself cannot (as an enamored subject) construct my love story to the end: I am its poet (its bard) only for the beginning; the end, like my own death, belongs to others; it is up to them to write the fiction, the external, mythic narrative.</i>
</blockquote><br><br>If you’ve read the relatively recent <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9279177-the-lover-s-dictionary" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Lover’s Dictionary</i>
</a> by David Levithan, you will see the similarity in structure. Whether you appreciated <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9279177-the-lover-s-dictionary" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Lover’s Dictionary</i>
</a> or not is immaterial, however, because Barthes’ classic masterpiece is a far, far cry from Levithan’s wordplay. Structurally, they both use fragments, of words or phrases explained, but <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/380994.A_Lover_s_Discourse" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>A Lover’s Discourse: Fragments</i>
</a> is more meaty and substantial. Reading it requires utmost concentration--you need to open your mind and your heart in order for it to penetrate. Only then will it enthrall you... captivate you. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380409907i/803070._SY540_.jpg" width="300" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="11238652276559656670" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2607302463" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2607302463" />
    <a title="Joshie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11973316-joshie"><img alt="Joshie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588954776p2/11973316.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2607302463?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joshie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joshie" href="/user/show/11973316-joshie">Joshie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099437420" href="/book/show/248326.A_Lover_s_Discourse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=literature-french">literature-french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=plays-and-essays">plays-and-essays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2607302463" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16370152516663031777">This extensive study of love has disemboweled me in every sense of the word. From Goethe's <i>Werther</i>, Freud's <i>Interpretation of Dreams</i>, Nietzsche's <i>The Gay Science</i>, Plato's <i>Symposium</i>, Proust's <i>In Search of Lost Time</i> to countless conversations with friends together with personal experiences Barthes painstakingly dissects love beyond the philosophical, psychological, and emotional. <i>A Lover's Discourse</i> bridges the resolute interstices between the head and the heart; bothering gestures and impressions</span>
  <span id="freeText16370152516663031777" style="display:none">This extensive study of love has disemboweled me in every sense of the word. From Goethe's <i>Werther</i>, Freud's <i>Interpretation of Dreams</i>, Nietzsche's <i>The Gay Science</i>, Plato's <i>Symposium</i>, Proust's <i>In Search of Lost Time</i> to countless conversations with friends together with personal experiences Barthes painstakingly dissects love beyond the philosophical, psychological, and emotional. <i>A Lover's Discourse</i> bridges the resolute interstices between the head and the heart; bothering gestures and impressions precipitating contradictions ** "Perpetual monologues apropos of a loved being, which are neither corrected nor nourished by that being, lead to erroneous notions concerning mutual relations, and make us strangers to each other when we meet again, so that we find things different from what, without realizing it, we imagined." (p159); the inane and the insane; the overthinking and overwhelming; the Image-repertoire.<br /><br />"Love is neither dialectical nor reformist."<br /><br />For most of us skeptic and insecure of ourselves in love, Barthes offers a place of solace and reflection in <i>A Lover's Discourse</i>. A heavy book of undeniable intensity, its secret is not so much in completely understanding the text but associating it with your own feelings and experiences of love and almost love. Indeed, love, although unfathomable, is a universal feeling. For the heartbroken, the confused, the frustrated, the mad, ** "The lover's solitude is not a solitude of person (love confides, speaks, tells itself), it is a solitude of system: I am alone in making a system out of it (perhaps because I am ceaselessly flung back on the solipsism of my discourse). A difficult paradox: I can be understood by everyone (love comes from books, its dialect is a common one), but I can be heard (received "prophetically") only by subjects who have <i>exactly and right now</i> the same language I have." (p212). All the naïvety, immaturity, ambiguity, and yearning: acknowledged and, to an extent, assuringly ordinary. It's all here, makes you feel better, relieved. And the drama in love cannot be separated from itself — love kills, can kill. Further, there is absolutely so much to take in from this. I unexpectedly gone through this quickly there is a weight on my chest as I look back on past love affairs with a different set of eyes. How much we have talked and wrote and depicted love that at times it seemed already overused, overhyped, yet it still interests, possesses, and arouses. Barthes strikes and alters. Highsmith put it memorably so: "Love was supposed to be a kind of blissful insanity."</span>
  <a data-text-id="16370152516663031777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18393509?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Corina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Corina" href="/user/show/1014996-corina">Corina</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18393509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2699058569134361386">The nerdiest book I reach for when falling in love or feeling heartbroken. A semiotic study of many moods and flavors of romantic love: <br/><br/>"Jealousy"<br/><br/>4. As a jealous man, I suffer four times over: because I am jealous, because I blame myself for being so, because I fear that my jealousy will wound the other, because I allow myself to be subject to a banality: I suffer from being excluded, from being aggressive, from being crazy, and from being common.<br/><br/>"The Uncertainty of Signs"<br/><i>whether he seeks to pro</i></span>
  <span id="freeText2699058569134361386" style="display:none">The nerdiest book I reach for when falling in love or feeling heartbroken. A semiotic study of many moods and flavors of romantic love: <br /><br />"Jealousy"<br /><br />4. As a jealous man, I suffer four times over: because I am jealous, because I blame myself for being so, because I fear that my jealousy will wound the other, because I allow myself to be subject to a banality: I suffer from being excluded, from being aggressive, from being crazy, and from being common.<br /><br />"The Uncertainty of Signs"<br /><i>whether he seeks to prove his love, or to discover if the other loves him, the amorous subject has no system of sure signs at his disposal.</i><br /><br />1. I look for signs, but of what? What is the object of my reading? Is it: am I loved (am I loved no longer, am I still loved)? Is it my future that I am trying to read, deciphering in what is inscribed the announcement of what will happen to me, according to a method which combines paleography and manticism? Isn't it rather, all things considered, that I remain suspended on this question whose answer I tirelessly seek in the other's face: <i>What am I worth? </i><br /><br />"Tenderness"<br /><br />1. There is not only the need for tenderness, there is also need to be tender for the other: we shut ourselves up in a mutual kindness, we mother each other reciprocally; we return to the root of all relations, where need and desire join. The tender gesture says: ask me anything that can put your body to sleep, but also do not forget that I desire you - a little, lightly, without trying to seize anything right away.<br /><br />Sexual pleasure is not metonymic: once taken, it is cut off: it was the Feast, always terminated and instituted only by temporary, supervised lifting of the prohibition. Tenderness, on the contrary, is nothing but an infinite, insatiable metonymy; the gesture, the episode of tenderness (the delicious harmony of an evening) can only be interrupted with laceration: everything seems called into question once again: return of rhythm - <i>vritti</i> - disappearance of <i>nirvana</i>.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/757995365" />

<span id="freeTextContainer11635236012098173746">I had one friend in particular-- I'm sure most of us have-- who, somewhere around his fifth drink, was vulnerable to going into the "why don't girls liiiiiiike me?" bitchfest, and, if interested in someone, "why doesn't (X) liiiiiike me as much as I liiiiiiike her?"<br/><br/>"Well, sir," I would have said had I read this book by then. "Roland might be a good guy for you to talk to. He'll tell you that if you're the sort of person who prevaricates over things and worries about the meanings of their words, </span>
  <span id="freeText11635236012098173746" style="display:none">I had one friend in particular-- I'm sure most of us have-- who, somewhere around his fifth drink, was vulnerable to going into the "why don't girls liiiiiiike me?" bitchfest, and, if interested in someone, "why doesn't (X) liiiiiike me as much as I liiiiiiike her?"<br /><br />"Well, sir," I would have said had I read this book by then. "Roland might be a good guy for you to talk to. He'll tell you that if you're the sort of person who prevaricates over things and worries about the meanings of their words, you'll have that same conversation with yourself when you're alone."<br /><br />That friend is more of a romantic than me, and so is Barthes. And being a responsible, emotionally honest, stable, faithful significant other is something I'm really not very good at. I've listened to both of these romantic souls, and incidentally primarily listened to both of them while perched on barstools. Neither of them will make me a better lover.<br /><br />But just like that same friend has my back for sure, Roland Barthes is someone I like to listen to, even when he's a man old enough to be my father who still compares himself to Werther.</span>

<div id="review_3181321547" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3181321547" />
    <a title="Konna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5850775-konna"><img alt="Konna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412957723p2/5850775.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3181321547?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Konna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Konna" href="/user/show/5850775-konna">Konna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099437420" href="/book/show/248326.A_Lover_s_Discourse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5850775-konna?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3181321547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9988609049667607901">Read full review on: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://thereadingarmchair.blogspot.com/2020/03/review-lovers-discourse.html'>https://thereadingarmchair.blogspot.c...</a><br/><br/>A Lover's Discourse is a book that I recently read and I have to say that it was a stimulating read. Before I go into details though, I will tell you of how I became aware of its existence. As I was browsing my social media (I know, I know) I came upon this wonderful quote that described the way that I was feeling at the moment. The quote went like this:<br/><br/>"Am I in love? -- yes, since I am waiting. The other one never waits. Sometimes I w</span>
  <span id="freeText9988609049667607901" style="display:none">Read full review on: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://thereadingarmchair.blogspot.com/2020/03/review-lovers-discourse.html">https://thereadingarmchair.blogspot.c...</a><br /><br />A Lover's Discourse is a book that I recently read and I have to say that it was a stimulating read. Before I go into details though, I will tell you of how I became aware of its existence. As I was browsing my social media (I know, I know) I came upon this wonderful quote that described the way that I was feeling at the moment. The quote went like this:<br /><br />"Am I in love? -- yes, since I am waiting. The other one never waits. Sometimes I want to play the part of the one who doesn't wait; I try to busy myself elsewhere, to arrive late; but I always lose at this game. Whatever I do, I find myself there, with nothing to do, punctual, even ahead of time. The lover's fatal identity is precisely this: I am the one who waits."<br /><br />However, this quote didn't have a source or anything (surprise!). So, I did the most logical next move - I Googled it. The result was A Lover's Discourse and I instantly decided to pick it up. Now, I had another reason that I wanted to read about love, and a Bathes' essay more specifically. <br /><br />I might have mentioned in the past that I was writing a novel. I have recently completed it and now I'm in the stage of editing it. Because my style is close to that of Japanese literature, my main character often shows things rather than saying them. For this reason, I needed some stimulants to fill my head with ideas that might produce that might help me in this endeavour. Now, I can safely say that this book was the ideal stimulant for my case.<br /><br />A Lover's Discourse (not to be confused with the YA novel The Lover's Dictionary) is a non-fiction book dedicated to the language of a lover. To be more precise, it is an essay on the expressions that a lover uses in his "outbursts of language" to explain his feelings. There are 80 small chapters in this book, each one dedicated to a different expression in the different phases of love. As Barthes underlines in his epilogue, he didn't place the chapter in an order that could potentially be interpreted into a story. However, if you really wanted to place them into a "correct" order to play out a story, you could easily do it.<br /><br />One thing that surprised me is that I have never thought that a person could put into writing all those different feelings. If you have been in love or had a crush on someone, you know that there is a great variety when it comes to the thoughts and feelings that take over you. Those feelings are not all exactly love or despair, as there are many stages in between. However impossible this might sound, Roland Barthes manages to do it. There were cases, where I had to stop reading because I was thinking of a moment that I felt exactly the same way the author described in that particular chapter.<br /><br />Having said that, it's time to talk about the language of A Lover's Discourse. This is not an easy read. There were times when I had to reread a certain part to understand its meaning. Furthermore, Roland Barthes uses a lot of references from various sources. However, I was not familiar with all of them. This didn't stop me from enjoying the book, even though I have a feeling that I might understand some parts better if I had an idea of what Barthes was actually talking about. Luckily for me, I had already read The Sorrows of Young Werther, which was heavily referenced in the book. Now, I simply feel that I want to reread Werther under the new light that A Lover's Discourse has given me.<br /><br />Another trap that I found in this book is that it is very quotable. How is this a trap? I could easily fill my review with quotes from it, without any other context, and you would LOVE it. Yes, it is great. Yes, it might be the book I have underlined the most (perhaps along with De Profundis). Nevertheless, I fear that because it is too quotable I might have stayed on the surface of the subject - at least in some parts.<br /><br />All in all, A Lover's Discourse was a challenging yet eye-opening book for me. I found it very relevant even though it was published in the '70s, especially now, with all the digital lovers and online dating. Just remember how many times you have been overanalyzing a text you received from your crush. A Lover's Discourse has given me a deeper understanding of this mystery that is love and all of its nuances. And with that, I'm leaving you with one more quote from the book:<br /><br />"I love the other, not according to his (accountable) qualities, but according to his existence; by a movement one might well call mystical, I love, not what he is, but that he is."</span>
  <a data-text-id="9988609049667607901" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer209235719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5779998478658678119">This is a powerful book.<br/><br/>Of all the philosophical graffiti written on the backs of bathroom doors when I was in college, my favorite was a simple survey: Are you in love or in love with the idea of love? Most people chose the latter.<br/><br/>Barthes tackles the depth and breadth of the idea of love, in all its agony and ecstasy. There are meditations on waiting, on jealousy, on how love at first sight is like rape. Barthes tackles the ideas of Werther, Nietzsche, Freud, and sprinkles the etymology of vari</span>
  <span id="freeText5779998478658678119" style="display:none">This is a powerful book.<br /><br />Of all the philosophical graffiti written on the backs of bathroom doors when I was in college, my favorite was a simple survey: Are you in love or in love with the idea of love? Most people chose the latter.<br /><br />Barthes tackles the depth and breadth of the idea of love, in all its agony and ecstasy. There are meditations on waiting, on jealousy, on how love at first sight is like rape. Barthes tackles the ideas of Werther, Nietzsche, Freud, and sprinkles the etymology of various Greek, French and Italian words for good measure. <br /><br />Just read this:<br /><br />This is how it happens sometimes, misery or joy engulfs me, without any particular tumult ensuing: nor any pathos: I am dissolved, not dismembered, I fall, I flow, I melt. Such thoughts grazed, touched, tested (the way you test the water with your foot) can recur.<br />Nothing solemn about them.<br />This is exactly what <i>gentleness</i> is. <br /><br />I was bowled over by this book. It is something to read again and again, and to ponder.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5779998478658678119" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2687500290?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anima" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anima" href="/user/show/59510944-anima">Anima</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782020046053" href="/book/show/300808.Fragments_d_un_discours_amoureux">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2687500290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7885550365453454878">" What do I think of love? -As a matter of fact, I think nothing at all of love. I'd be glad to know what it is, but being inside, I see it in existence, not in essence. What I want to know (love) is the very substance I employ in order to speak (the lover's discourse) . Reflection is certainly permitted, but since this reflection is immediately absorbed in the mulling over of images, it never turns into<br/>reflexivity: excluded from logic (which supposes languages exterior to each other), I cannot </span>
  <span id="freeText7885550365453454878" style="display:none">" What do I think of love? -As a matter of fact, I think nothing at all of love. I'd be glad to know what it is, but being inside, I see it in existence, not in essence. What I want to know (love) is the very substance I employ in order to speak (the lover's discourse) . Reflection is certainly permitted, but since this reflection is immediately absorbed in the mulling over of images, it never turns into<br />reflexivity: excluded from logic (which supposes languages exterior to each other), I cannot claim to think properly. Hence, discourse on love though I may for years at a time, I cannot hope to seize the concept of it except "by the tail": by flashes, formulas, surprises of expression, scattered through the great stream of the Image-repertoire; I am in love's wrong place, which is its dazzling place :<br />"The darkest place, according to a Chinese proverb, is always underneath the lamp."<br /><br />"Laetitia<br />....<br />1. Cicero, and later Leibnitz, opposes gaudium to laetitia. Gaudium is " the pleasure the soul experiences when it considers the possession of a present or future good as assured; and we are in possession of such a good when it is in such a way within our power that we can enjoy it when we wish."<br /> Laetitia is a lively pleasure, "a state in which pleasure predominates within us" (among<br />other, often contradictory sensations).<br />Gaudium is what I dream of: to enjoy a lifelong pleasure. But being unable to accede to Gaudium, from which I am separated by a thousand obstacles, I dream of falling back on Laetitia: if I could manage to confine myself to the lively pleasures the other affords me, without contaminating them, mortifying them by the anxiety which serves as their hinge? ..'</span>
  <a data-text-id="7885550365453454878" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3066395459" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3066395459" />
    <a title="Thomas Feng" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32818031-thomas-feng"><img alt="Thomas Feng" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562083817p2/32818031.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3066395459?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas Feng" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas Feng" href="/user/show/32818031-thomas-feng">Thomas Feng</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3066395459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3224342464122294699">"and in France they say, every day, love puts on a new face" – Joni Mitchell<br/><br/>as with so much great art, what I know I will remember from this book is not so much its "insights" about love (of which there are many... perhaps even a surfeit), but more that it proposes a new framework of which to think about love altogether. Barthes's observations and approach of close-reading provides us a new way to observe and close-read for ourselves. <br/><br/>I like to think this is part of what he means when he says th</span>
  <span id="freeText3224342464122294699" style="display:none">"and in France they say, every day, love puts on a new face" – Joni Mitchell<br /><br />as with so much great art, what I know I will remember from this book is not so much its "insights" about love (of which there are many... perhaps even a surfeit), but more that it proposes a new framework of which to think about love altogether. Barthes's observations and approach of close-reading provides us a new way to observe and close-read for ourselves. <br /><br />I like to think this is part of what he means when he says that reading is a cooperative act: the text means nothing if we do not make our own meaning for it (to my mind, the premise of art). curiously, the approach he takes is to use the rhetoric of logical ("objective") exegesis (each figure carries the tone/forcefulness of an argument), though the intent seems to be to describe. and so taking each figure at face value could be frustrating, at times feeling like the overzealous unpacking of a cliché – in an attempt to provide *insight*, merely a very fancy way of stating the obvious. (...maybe that's what we call poetry.)<br /><br />I enjoyed the book most when I could read it as poetry rather than as argument. could I say I could project myself into each of the figures? I mean, "I guess", but that's not what I find most beautiful about the book. it's the lyrical approach to thought, and the clear-minded, courageous reckoning with love as its own altered state, its own idiosyncratic structure of cognition. in its detail it even exfoliates platitudes, it reminds me of how truly strange love really is, it opens possibilities of feeling more vividly and perhaps more deeply.<br /><br />"I want to change systems: no longer to unmask, no longer to interpret, but to make consciousness itself a drug, and thereby to accede to the perfect vision of reality..." – A Lover's Discourse</span>
  <a data-text-id="3224342464122294699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2314030270" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2314030270" />
    <a title="Mél ☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50239207-m-l"><img alt="Mél ☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529686111p2/50239207.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2314030270?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mél ☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mél ☽" href="/user/show/50239207-m-l">Mél ☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Language lovers, People who could neither get nor get over their Charlotte. </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50239207-m-l?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50239207-m-l?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50239207-m-l?shelf=theory">theory</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2314030270" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8386677811609143595">Inexpressible Love<br/><br/>Écrire / to write<br/><br/>Enticements, arguments, and impasses generated by the desire to “express” amorous feeling in a “creation” (particularly of writing)<br/> --------------------<br/><br/>For as long as I can remember, few ever dared to define love, and those who did, have would always been met by failure. <br/>Now, Roland Barthes, here, takes this sublime feeling, and dips it in the realm of language, so slowly, so elegantly, clothing its bare skin with written symbols, and breathing meaning into its</span>
  <span id="freeText8386677811609143595" style="display:none">Inexpressible Love<br /><br />Écrire / to write<br /><br />Enticements, arguments, and impasses generated by the desire to “express” amorous feeling in a “creation” (particularly of writing)<br /> --------------------<br /><br />For as long as I can remember, few ever dared to define love, and those who did, have would always been met by failure. <br />Now, Roland Barthes, here, takes this sublime feeling, and dips it in the realm of language, so slowly, so elegantly, clothing its bare skin with written symbols, and breathing meaning into its lungs. <br />Love becomes, theatrical, planned; a structured “aesthetic creation”. Every motion serves a purpose, and every stillness delivers deliberation. <br />--------<br /> For someone who is ardently, passionately, dangerously in love, this book said all the things I could never have. For I have watched my own amorous expressions be created, and recreated to the mere whispers tiptoeing behind my loved object’s phenomenon. And I have suffered in the harsh folds of both her existence and her absence, I have worshiped my version of her “Blue Coat and Yellow Vest”, as I have cursed my own failure at even failing to snap out of it, or to drown, wholly.<br />--------<br />And if I cannot have Her, then, “I shall produce an immortal work by writing my passion”<br />--------<br />I do realize this book -or any other book- must be reviewed according to what it presented, rather than the things it reminded me of, yet, I find it most difficult to establish a separation between the images and symbols dealt with, and my very own amorous experience. Such a literary work -not to say a novel- aims at creating linkages, and not abolishing them. Language is a collective experience, and HERE is the starkest of proofs. [You only know that love is Love, because you know it’s a feeling, it is a suffering when unrequited. Also, because you know the differences between Love, and mere admiration, or Love and friendship.] <br />--------<br />By and by, I have come to the realization that I need to find someone to write my love story for me. <br /><br />"Novel/Drama<br />Drame / Drama<br />The amorous subject cannot write his love story himself.”<br /><br />PS: I would love to read a review by someone who is not in love. It would be very interesting. I'll be certainly rereading this marvelous work, two years from now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8386677811609143595" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_308576324" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Jafar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26188-jafar"><img alt="Jafar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407873633p2/26188.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jafar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jafar" href="/user/show/26188-jafar">Jafar</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer308576324" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2180607393771816101"><blockquote>cacher / to hide<br/>A deliberative figure: the amorous subject wonders, not whether he should declare his love to the loved being (this is not a figure of avowal), but to what degree he should conceal the turbulences of his passion: his desires, his distresses; in short, his excesses (in Racinian language: his <i>fureur</i>).<br/><br/>Yet to hide a passion totally (or even to hide, more simply, its excess) is inconceivable: not because the human subject is too weak, but because passion is in essence made to be seen:</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText2180607393771816101" style="display:none"><blockquote>cacher / to hide<br />A deliberative figure: the amorous subject wonders, not whether he should declare his love to the loved being (this is not a figure of avowal), but to what degree he should conceal the turbulences of his passion: his desires, his distresses; in short, his excesses (in Racinian language: his <i>fureur</i>).<br /><br />Yet to hide a passion totally (or even to hide, more simply, its excess) is inconceivable: not because the human subject is too weak, but because passion is in essence made to be seen: the hiding must be seen: <i>I want you to know that I am hiding something from you</i>, that is the active paradox I must resolve: <i>at one and the same time</i> it must be known and not known: I want you to know that I don’t want to show my feelings: that is the message I address to the other. <i>Larvatus prodeo</i>: I advance pointing to my mask: I set a mask upon my passion, but with a discreet (and wily) finger I designate this mask. Every passion, ultimately, has its spectator: at the moment of his death, Captain Paz cannot keep from writing to the woman he has loved in silence: no amorous oblation without a final theater: the sign is always victorious.</blockquote><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2180607393771816101" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5116041936610487442">Uffffff. Maybe the most quotable book I've ever read. The number of times I had to pause and ponder is innumerable. It highlights, totally, the state of the lover with regard to the beloved and how melancholy and joy are closely linked in love. This is the work of genius.<br/><br/>"Am I in love? --yes, since I am waiting. The other one never waits. Sometimes I want to play the part of the one who doesn't wait; I try to busy myself elsewhere, to arrive late; but I always lose at this game. Whatever I do, I</span>
  <span id="freeText5116041936610487442" style="display:none">Uffffff. Maybe the most quotable book I've ever read. The number of times I had to pause and ponder is innumerable. It highlights, totally, the state of the lover with regard to the beloved and how melancholy and joy are closely linked in love. This is the work of genius.<br /><br />"Am I in love? --yes, since I am waiting. The other one never waits. Sometimes I want to play the part of the one who doesn't wait; I try to busy myself elsewhere, to arrive late; but I always lose at this game. Whatever I do, I find myself there, with nothing to do, punctual, even ahead of time. The lover's fatal identity is precisely this: I am the one who waits."<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16485274487526765466">This is perhaps Barthes at his most playful as he anatomises the textuality of love. Love, he asserts, is articulated, expressed and performed via a series of 'codes' or an 'image repertoire' - and his analysis takes in not just the individual and the personal (noted by initials in the margins) but the universal and general as depicted in narratives from Plato to Lacan via Goethe, Balzac and Proust, to name just a few.<br/><br/>Despite the mischievous postmoderness, this is also very good on the cruel dec</span>
  <span id="freeText16485274487526765466" style="display:none">This is perhaps Barthes at his most playful as he anatomises the textuality of love. Love, he asserts, is articulated, expressed and performed via a series of 'codes' or an 'image repertoire' - and his analysis takes in not just the individual and the personal (noted by initials in the margins) but the universal and general as depicted in narratives from Plato to Lacan via Goethe, Balzac and Proust, to name just a few.<br /><br />Despite the mischievous postmoderness, this is also very good on the cruel deceptions of our self-told love stories and is perhaps Barthes at his most human and accessible.</span>
